<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:313px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="428" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif/311px-Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif/467px-Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif/622px-Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif 2x" width="311"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Non-Native_American_Nations_Control_over_South_America_1700_and_on.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>European <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">states</a> claimed sovereignty over <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, 1700</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_South_America.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="493" decoding="async" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Map_of_South_America.png/300px-Map_of_South_America.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Map_of_South_America.png/450px-Map_of_South_America.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Map_of_South_America.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_South_America.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Contemporary political map of South America</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>history of South America</b> is the study of the past, particularly the written record, oral histories, and traditions, passed down from generation to generation on the continent of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>. The continent continues to be home to indigenous peoples, some of whom built high civilizations prior to the arrival of Europeans in the late 1400s and early 1500s. South America has a history that has a wide range of human cultures and forms of civilization. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norte_Chico_civilization" title="Norte Chico civilization">Norte Chico civilization</a> in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> is the oldest civilization in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and one of the first six independent civilizations in the world; it was contemporaneous with the Egyptian pyramids. It predated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesoamerican" title="Mesoamerican">Mesoamerican</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">Olmec</a> by nearly two millennia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Millennia of independent indigenous existence was disrupted by European colonization from Spain and Portugal, and by demographic collapse. However, the resulting culture both in continent's <i>mestizos</i>, and in indigenous cultures remained quite distinct from those of their colonizers. Through the trans-Atlantic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>, South America (especially Brazil) became the home of millions of people of the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African diaspora</a>. The mixing of ethnic groups led to new social structures.
</p><p>The tensions between Europeans, indigenous peoples, and African slaves and their descendants shaped South America, starting in sixteenth century. Most of Spanish America achieved its independence in the early nineteenth century through hard fought <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">wars</a>, while Portuguese Brazil first became the seat of the Portuguese empire and then an empire independent of Portugal. With the revolution for independence from the Spanish crown achieved during the 19th century, South America underwent yet more social and political changes. These have included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nation_building" title="Nation building">nation building</a> projects, absorbing waves of immigration from Europe in the late 19th and 20th centuries, dealing with increased international trade, colonization of hinterlands, and wars about territory ownership and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_balance" title="Power balance">power balance</a>. During this period there has also been the reorganization of Indigenous rights and duties, subjugation of Indigenous peoples living in the states' frontiers, that lasted until the early 1900s; liberal-conservative conflicts among the ruling classes, and major demographic and environmental changes accompanying the development of sensitive habitats.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_CIAT_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2632" data-file-width="4123" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg/280px-Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg/420px-Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg/560px-Amazon_CIAT_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazon_CIAT_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of the Amazon rainforest, near <a href="/wiki/Manaus" title="Manaus">Manaus</a></div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Settlement_of_the_Americas" title="Settlement of the Americas">Settlement of the Americas</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blakey_105Ma_-_COL.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="216" data-file-width="273" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Blakey_105Ma_-_COL.jpg/250px-Blakey_105Ma_-_COL.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Blakey_105Ma_-_COL.jpg 1.5x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blakey_105Ma_-_COL.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paleogeography of northern South America around 105 mya</div></div></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Paleozoic" title="Paleozoic">Paleozoic</a> and Early <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> eras, <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> were connected in a landmass called <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a>, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Supercontinent" title="Supercontinent">supercontinent</a> <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Albian" title="Albian">Albian</a>, around 110 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>, South America and Africa began to diverge along the southern <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a>, giving rise to a landmass of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and South America. During the late <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>, around 35 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>, Antarctica and South America separated and South America became a massive, biologically rich island-continent. During approximately 30 million years, the <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> of South America was isolated from the rest of the world, leading to the evolution of species within the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1988_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1988-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">event that caused the mass-extinction of dinosaurs 66 Mya</a> gave rise to <a href="/wiki/Neotropical_realm" title="Neotropical realm">neotropical</a> <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a> <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a> like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazonia" title="Amazonia">Amazonia</a>, replacing species composition and structure of local forests. During <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">~6 million years of recovery</a> to former levels of plant <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">diversity</a>, they evolved from widely spaced <a href="/wiki/Gymnosperm" title="Gymnosperm">gymnosperm</a>-dominated forests to the forests with thick <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopies</a> which block sunlight, prevalent <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a> and high <a href="/wiki/Stratification_(vegetation)" title="Stratification (vegetation)">vertical layering</a> as known today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1126/science.abf1969_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1126/science.abf1969-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Geological evidence suggests that approximately 3 million years ago, South America became connected to North America when the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bolivar_Trough&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bolivar Trough (page does not exist)">Bolivar Trough</a> marine barrier disappeared and the Panamanian land bridge formed. The joining of these two land masses led to the <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Interchange" title="Great American Interchange">Great American Interchange</a>, in which biota from both continents expanded their ranges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1988_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1988-1">[1]</a></sup> The first species known to have made the northward migration was <i><a href="/wiki/Pliometanastes" title="Pliometanastes">Pliometanastes</a></i>, a fossil <a href="/wiki/Ground_sloth" title="Ground sloth">ground sloth</a> roughly the size of a modern <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">black bear</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1988_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1988-1">[1]</a></sup> Migrations to the Southern Hemisphere were undertaken by several <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North American</a> mammalian carnivores. Fewer species migrated in the opposite direction from south to north. The result of the expansion of a North American fauna was a mass extinction in which hundreds of species disappeared in a relatively short time. About 60% of present-day South American mammals have evolved from North American species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1988_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1988-1">[1]</a></sup> Some South American species were able to adapt and spread into North America. Apart from <i>Pliometanastes</i>, during the <a href="/wiki/Irvingtonian" title="Irvingtonian">Irvingtonian</a> stage of the mammal land stages, around 1.9 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>, species as  <i><a href="/wiki/Pampatherium" title="Pampatherium">Pampatherium</a></i>, a giant <a href="/wiki/Armadillo" title="Armadillo">armadillo</a>, ground sloth <i><a href="/wiki/Megatherium" title="Megatherium">Megatherium</a></i>, giant <a href="/wiki/Anteater" title="Anteater">anteater</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myrmecophaga" title="Myrmecophaga">Myrmecophaga</a></i>, a Neogene <a href="/wiki/Capybara" title="Capybara">capybara</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Hydrochoerus" title="Hydrochoerus">Hydrochoerus</a></i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Meizonyx" title="Meizonyx">Meizonyx</a></i>, opossum <i><a href="/wiki/Didelphis" title="Didelphis">Didelphis</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mixotoxodon" title="Mixotoxodon">Mixotoxodon</a></i> followed the route north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1988_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1988-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terror_bird" title="Terror bird">terror bird</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Titanis" title="Titanis">Titanis</a></i>, the only large carnivore in South American, dispersed into North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall1988_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall1988-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Columbian_era">Pre-Columbian era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Columbian era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era#South_America" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Columbian era § South America</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_domestication_of_animals">Agriculture and domestication of animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Agriculture and domestication of animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Americas are thought to have been first inhabited by people from eastern Asia who crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bering_Land_Bridge" title="Bering Land Bridge">Bering Land Bridge</a> to present-day Alaska; the land separated and the continents are divided by the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Strait</a>. Over the course of millennia, three waves of migrants spread to all parts of the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Genetic and linguistic evidence has shown that the last wave of migrant peoples settled across the northern tier, and did not reach South America.
</p><p>The first evidence for the existence of agricultural practices in South America dates back to circa 6500 BCE, when <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">chilies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bean" title="Bean">beans</a> began to be cultivated for food in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">Amazon Basin</a>. Pottery evidence suggests that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a>, which remains a staple foodstuff today, was being cultivated as early as 2000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>South American cultures began domesticating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Llamas" title="Llamas">llamas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpacas" title="Alpacas">alpacas</a> in the highlands of the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> circa 3500 BCE. These animals were used for both transportation and meat; their fur was shorn or collected to use to make clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25-5">[5]</a></sup> Guinea pigs were also domesticated as a food source at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 2000 BCE, many agrarian village communities had developed throughout the Andes and the surrounding regions. Fishing became a widespread practice along the coast, with fish being the primary source of food for those communities. Irrigation systems were also developed at this time, which aided in the rise of agrarian societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25-5">[5]</a></sup> The food crops were <a href="/wiki/Quinoa" title="Quinoa">quinoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lima_bean" title="Lima bean">lima beans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_bean" title="Common bean">common beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">sweet potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxalis_tuberosa" title="Oxalis tuberosa">oca</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Squash_(plant)" title="Squash (plant)">squashes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">Cotton</a> was also grown and was particularly important as the only major fiber crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-O'Brien,_Patrick_2005._pp._25-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among the earliest permanent settlements, dated to 4700 BC is the <a href="/wiki/Huaca_Prieta" title="Huaca Prieta">Huaca Prieta</a> site on the coast of Peru, and at 3500 BC the <a href="/wiki/Valdivia_culture" title="Valdivia culture">Valdivia culture</a> in Ecuador. Other groups also formed permanent settlements. Among those groups were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muisca_people" title="Muisca people">Muisca</a> or "Muysca," and the Tairona, located in present-day Colombia. The <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1ari" title="Cañari">Cañari</a> of Ecuador, <a href="/wiki/Quechua_people" title="Quechua people">Quechua</a> of Peru, and <a href="/wiki/Aymara_people" title="Aymara people">Aymara</a> of Bolivia were the three most important Native peoples who developed societies of sedentary agriculture in South America. 
</p><p>In the last two thousand years, there may have been contact with the <a href="/wiki/Polynesia" title="Polynesia">Polynesians</a> who sailed to and from the continent across the South Pacific Ocean. The sweet potato, which originated in South America, <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato#Origin,_distribution_and_diversity" title="Sweet potato">spread</a> through some areas of the Pacific. There is no genetic legacy of human contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_activity">Human activity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Human activity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="699" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg/220px-%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg/330px-%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg/440px-%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%C3%8Dndios_isolados_no_Acre_5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of an uncontacted tribe encountered in the <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazilian</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Acre_(state)" title="Acre (state)">Acre</a> in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest archaeological evidence from human settlement comes from <a href="/wiki/Monte_Verde" title="Monte Verde">Monte Verde</a> (possibly as early as 16,500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillehay_2015_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dillehay_2015-9">[9]</a></sup> Based on <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> evidence from an excavation at <a href="/wiki/Caverna_da_Pedra_Pintada" title="Caverna da Pedra Pintada">Caverna da Pedra Pintada</a>, human inhabitants first settled in the Amazon region at least 11,200 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fazenda_Colorada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="344" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fazenda_Colorada.jpg/180px-Fazenda_Colorada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fazenda_Colorada.jpg/270px-Fazenda_Colorada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Fazenda_Colorada.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fazenda_Colorada.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geoglyphs on deforested land in the Amazon rainforest, Acre</div></div></div>
<p>For a long time it was thought that the Amazon rainforest was only ever sparsely populated, as it was impossible to sustain a large population through <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> given the poor soil. Archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Betty_Meggers" title="Betty Meggers">Betty Meggers</a> was a prominent proponent of this idea, as described in her book <i>Amazonia: Man and Culture in a Counterfeit Paradise</i>. She claimed that a population density of 0.2 inhabitants per square kilometre (0.52/sq mi) is the maximum that can be sustained in the rainforest through hunting, with agriculture needed to support a larger population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> However, recent archaeological findings have suggested that the region was actually densely populated. From the 1970s, numerous <a href="/wiki/Geoglyph" title="Geoglyph">geoglyphs</a> have been discovered on deforested land dating between 0–1250<span class="nowrap"> </span>CE, leading to claims about <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Columbian</a> civilisations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_Romero_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simon_Romero-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Norte_Chico">Norte Chico</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Norte Chico">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caral_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1584" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Caral_1.JPG/220px-Caral_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Caral_1.JPG/330px-Caral_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Caral_1.JPG/440px-Caral_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caral_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caral" title="Caral">Caral</a></div></div></div>
<p>On the north-central coast of present-day <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norte_Chico_civilization" title="Norte Chico civilization">Norte Chico civilization</a> emerged as one of six civilizations to develop independently in the world. It was roughly contemporaneous with the Egyptian pyramids. It preceded the civilization of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a> by two millennia. It is believed to have been the only civilization dependent on fishing rather than agriculture to support its population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Caral" title="Caral">Caral Supe</a> complex is one of the larger Norte Chico sites and has been dated to 27th century BCE. It is noteworthy for having absolutely no signs of warfare. It was contemporary with urbanism's rise in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Ca.C3.B1ari"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cañari">Cañari</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cañari">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg/220px-Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg/330px-Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg/440px-Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ingapirca_Canari_structures.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ingapirca" title="Ingapirca">Ingapirca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, Caħari ruins: astronomical stone (left), tomb (right) and reconstructed house (background)</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1ari" title="Cañari">Cañari</a> were the indigenous natives of today's Ecuadorian provinces of <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1ar_Province" title="Cañar Province">Cañar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azuay_Province" title="Azuay Province">Azuay</a> at the time of European contact. They were an elaborate civilization with advanced architecture and religious belief. Most of their remains were either burned or destroyed from attacks by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca" title="Inca">Inca</a> and later the Spaniards. Their old city "Guapondelig", was replaced twice, first by the Incan city of Tomipamba, and later by the colonial city of <a href="/wiki/Cuenca,_Ecuador" title="Cuenca, Ecuador">Cuenca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The city was believed by the Spanish to be the site of <a href="/wiki/El_Dorado" title="El Dorado">El Dorado</a>, the city of gold from the mythology of Colombia.
</p><p>The Cañari were most notable in having repulsed the Incan invasion with fierce resistance for many years until they fell to Tupac Yupanqui. It is said that the Inca strategically married the Cañari princess Paccha to conquer the people. Many of their descendants still reside in <a href="/wiki/Ca%C3%B1ar_Province" title="Cañar Province">Cañar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chibchan_Nations">Chibchan Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chibchan Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chibchan_languages_distribution.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="784" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Chibchan_languages_distribution.png/250px-Chibchan_languages_distribution.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Chibchan_languages_distribution.png/375px-Chibchan_languages_distribution.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Chibchan_languages_distribution.png/500px-Chibchan_languages_distribution.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chibchan_languages_distribution.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Distribution of <a href="/wiki/Chibcha_language" title="Chibcha language">Chibchan languages</a> in southern Central America and northwestern South America, present-day Colombia</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Chibchan_Nations" title="Spanish conquest of the Chibchan Nations">Spanish conquest of the Chibchan Nations</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Chibcha_language" title="Chibcha language">Chibcha-speaking</a> communities were the most numerous, the most extended by territory, and the most socio-economically developed of the Pre-Hispanic Colombian cultures. They were divided into two linguistic subgroups; the Arwako-Chimila languages, with the <a href="/wiki/Tairona" title="Tairona">Tairona</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kankuamo_people" title="Kankuamo people">Kankuamo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kogi_people" title="Kogi people">Kogi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arhuaco_people" title="Arhuaco people">Arhuaco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chimila_people" title="Chimila people">Chimila</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chitarero_people" title="Chitarero people">Chitarero people</a> and the Kuna-Colombian languages with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuna_people" title="Kuna people">Kuna</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutabes_people" title="Nutabes people">Nutabe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motilon_people" title="Motilon people">Motilon</a>, <a href="/wiki/U%27wa_people" title="U'wa people">U'wa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lache_people" title="Lache people">Lache</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guane_people" title="Guane people">Guane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sutagao_people" title="Sutagao people">Sutagao</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muisca_people" title="Muisca people">Muisca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlottologChibchan_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlottologChibchan-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Muisca">Muisca</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Muisca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3350" data-file-width="5176" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg/250px-Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg/375px-Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg/500px-Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Muisca_Fine_Golden_Figures_-_Museo_del_Oro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The fine goldworking of the Muisca formed the basis for the expedition from the Caribbean coast into the heart of the Andes, where they developed an advanced civilisation based on <a href="/wiki/Muisca_agriculture" title="Muisca agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Muisca_society" title="Women in Muisca society">salt production</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muisca_economy#Trade" title="Muisca economy">trade</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muisca_people" title="Muisca people">Muisca people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Muisca_Confederation" title="Muisca Confederation">Muisca Confederation</a></div>
<p>Of these indigenous groups, the Muisca were the most advanced and formed one of the four grand civilisations in the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> With the <a href="/wiki/Inca_Empire" title="Inca Empire">Inca</a> in Peru, they constituted the two developed and specialised societies of South America. The Muisca, meaning "people" or "person" in their version of the Chibcha language; <i>Muysccubun</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> inhabited the <a href="/wiki/Altiplano_Cundiboyacense" title="Altiplano Cundiboyacense">Altiplano Cundiboyacense</a>, the high plateau in the <a href="/wiki/Cordillera_Oriental_(Colombia)" title="Cordillera Oriental (Colombia)">Eastern Ranges</a> of the Colombian <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> and surrounding valleys, such as the <a href="/wiki/Tenza_Valley" title="Tenza Valley">Tenza Valley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-websiteTenza_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-websiteTenza-20">[20]</a></sup> Commonly set at 800 AD, their history succeeded the <a href="/wiki/Herrera_Period" title="Herrera Period">Herrera Period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The people were organised in a loose confederation of <a href="/wiki/Muisca_rulers" title="Muisca rulers">rulers</a>, later called the <a href="/wiki/Muisca_Confederation" title="Muisca Confederation">Muisca Confederation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gamboa_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gamboa-22">[22]</a></sup> At the time of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Muisca" title="Spanish conquest of the Muisca">Spanish conquest</a>, their reign spread across the modern departments <a href="/wiki/Cundinamarca_Department" title="Cundinamarca Department">Cundinamarca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boyac%C3%A1_Department" title="Boyacá Department">Boyacá</a> with small parts of southern <a href="/wiki/Santander_Department" title="Santander Department">Santander</a> with a surface area of approximately 25,000 square kilometres (9,700 sq mi) and a total population of between 300,000 and two million individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pop_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-pop-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Muisca were known as "The Salt People", thanks to their extraction of and trade in <a href="/wiki/Halite" title="Halite">halite</a> from brines in various salt mines of which those in <a href="/wiki/Zipaquir%C3%A1" title="Zipaquirá">Zipaquirá</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nemoc%C3%B3n" title="Nemocón">Nemocón</a> are still the most important. This extraction process was the work of the <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Muisca_society" title="Women in Muisca society">Muisca women</a> exclusively and formed the backbone of their highly regarded <a href="/wiki/Muisca_economy#Trade" title="Muisca economy">trading</a> with other Chibcha-, Arawak- and Cariban-speaking neighboring indigenous groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daza_p23_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Daza_p23-27">[27]</a></sup> Trading was performed using salt, small cotton cloths and larger mantles and ceramics as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barter_trade" title="Barter trade">barter trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Francis_p44_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Francis_p44-28">[28]</a></sup> Their economy was agricultural in nature, profiting from the fertile soils of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Humboldt" title="Lake Humboldt">Lake Humboldt</a> that existed on the Bogotá savanna until around 30,000 years BP. Their crops were cultivated using irrigation and drainage on elevated terraces and mounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daza_p23_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Daza_p23-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> To the Spanish conquistadors they were best known for their advanced <a href="/wiki/Muisca_economy" title="Muisca economy">gold-working</a>, as represented in the <i><a href="/wiki/Tunjo" title="Tunjo">tunjos</a></i> (votive offer pieces), spread in <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muisca_museum_collections" title="List of Muisca museum collections">museum collections</a> all around the world. The famous <a href="/wiki/Muisca_raft" title="Muisca raft">Muisca raft</a>, centerpiece in the collection of the <a href="/wiki/Gold_Museum,_Bogot%C3%A1" title="Gold Museum, Bogotá">Museo del Oro</a> in the Colombian capital <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">Bogotá</a>, shows the skilled goldworking of the inhabitants of the Altiplano. The Muisca were the only pre-Columbian civilization known in South America to have used coins (<i>tejuelos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gold and <i><a href="/wiki/Tumbaga" title="Tumbaga">tumbaga</a></i> (a gold-silver-copper alloy elaborated by the Muisca) created the legend of <i><a href="/wiki/El_Dorado" title="El Dorado">El Dorado</a></i>; the "land, city or man of gold". The Spanish conquistadors who landed in the Caribbean city of <a href="/wiki/Santa_Marta" title="Santa Marta">Santa Marta</a> were informed of the rich gold culture and led by <a href="/wiki/Gonzalo_Jim%C3%A9nez_de_Quesada" title="Gonzalo Jiménez de Quesada">Gonzalo Jiménez de Quesada</a> and his brother <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_P%C3%A9rez_de_Quesada" title="Hernán Pérez de Quesada">Hernán Pérez</a>, organised the most strenuous of the Spanish conquests into the heart of the Andes in April 1536. After an expedition of a year, where 80% of the soldiers died due to the harsh climate, carnivores such as caimans and <a href="/wiki/Jaguar" title="Jaguar">jaguars</a> and the frequent attacks of the indigenous peoples found along the route, <a href="/wiki/Tisquesusa" title="Tisquesusa">Tisquesusa</a>, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zipa" title="Zipa">zipa</a></i> of Bacatá, on the <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1_savanna" title="Bogotá savanna">Bogotá savanna</a>, was beaten by the Spanish on April 20, 1537, and died "bathing in his own blood", as prophesied by the <a href="/wiki/Mohan_(legendary)" title="Mohan (legendary)">mohan</a> Popón.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amazon">Amazon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Amazon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rainforest#History" title="Amazon rainforest">Amazon rainforest § History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chachapoya_culture" title="Chachapoya culture">Chachapoya culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fazenda_Colorada.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="344" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fazenda_Colorada.jpg/150px-Fazenda_Colorada.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fazenda_Colorada.jpg/225px-Fazenda_Colorada.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Fazenda_Colorada.jpg/300px-Fazenda_Colorada.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fazenda_Colorada.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Geoglyphs on deforested land in the Amazon rainforest</div></div></div>
<p>For a long time, scholars believed that Amazon forests were occupied by small numbers of hunter-gatherer tribes. Archeologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Betty_J._Meggers" title="Betty J. Meggers">Betty J. Meggers</a> was a prominent proponent of this idea, as described in her book <i>Amazonia: Man and Culture in a Counterfeit Paradise</i>. However, recent archeological findings have suggested that the region was densely populated. From the 1970s, numerous <a href="/wiki/Geoglyph" title="Geoglyph">geoglyphs</a> have been discovered on deforested land dating between 0–1250 AD. Additional finds have led to conclusions that there were highly developed and populous cultures in the forests, organized as <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Columbian</a> civilizations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simon_Romero_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simon_Romero-12">[12]</a></sup> The BBC's <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unnatural_Histories_(TV_series)" title="Unnatural Histories (TV series)">Unnatural Histories</a></i> claimed that the Amazon rainforest, rather than being a pristine <a href="/wiki/Wilderness" title="Wilderness">wilderness</a>, has been shaped by man for at least 11,000 years through practices such as <a href="/wiki/Forest_gardening" title="Forest gardening">forest gardening</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first European to travel the length of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_River" title="Amazon River">Amazon River</a> was <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Orellana" title="Francisco de Orellana">Francisco de Orellana</a> in 1542.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The BBC documentary <i>Unnatural Histories</i> presents evidence that Francisco de Orellana, rather than exaggerating his claims as previously thought, was correct in his observations that an advanced civilization was flourishing along the Amazon in the 1540s. It is believed that the civilization was later devastated by the spread of infectious diseases from Europe, such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, to which the natives had no immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc-33">[33]</a></sup> Some 5 million people may have lived in the Amazon region in 1500, divided between dense coastal settlements, such as that at <a href="/wiki/Marajoara_culture" title="Marajoara culture">Marajó</a>, and inland dwellers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-park_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-park-35">[35]</a></sup> By 1900 the population had fallen to 1 million, and by the early 1980s, it was less than 200,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-park_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-park-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers have found that the fertile <i><a href="/wiki/Terra_preta" title="Terra preta">terra preta</a></i> (black earth) is distributed over large areas in the Amazon forest. It is now widely accepted that these soils are a product of indigenous <a href="/wiki/Soil_management" title="Soil management">soil management</a>. The development of this soil enabled <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silviculture" title="Silviculture">silviculture</a> to be conducted in the previously hostile environment. Large portions of the Amazon rainforest are therefore probably the result of centuries of human management, rather than naturally occurring as has previously been supposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> In the region of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xinguanos" title="Xinguanos">Xinguanos</a> tribe, remains of some of these large, mid-forest Amazon settlements were found in 2003 by Michael Heckenberger and colleagues of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Florida" title="University of Florida">University of Florida</a>. Among those remains were evidence of constructed roads, bridges and large plazas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Andean_civilizations">Andean civilizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Andean civilizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span id="Chav.C3.ADn"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chavín">Chavín</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Chavín">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_culture" title="Chavín culture">Chavín</a>, a South American preliterate civilization, established a trade network and developed agriculture by 900 BCE, according to some estimates and archeological finds. Artifacts were found at a site called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Huantar" title="Chavín de Huantar">Chavín de Huantar</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a> at an elevation of 3,177 meters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Chavín civilization spanned 900 to 200 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Moche">Moche</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Moche">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moche_jt01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Moche_jt01.jpg/220px-Moche_jt01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Moche_jt01.jpg/330px-Moche_jt01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Moche_jt01.jpg/440px-Moche_jt01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moche_jt01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Moche,_Trujillo" title="Moche, Trujillo">Moche</a> with the <i>"Huaca del Sol"</i> in the background</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moche_(culture)" title="Moche (culture)">Moche</a> thrived on the north coast of Peru between the first and ninth century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The heritage of the Moche comes down to us through their elaborate burials, excavated by former UCLA professor <a href="/wiki/Christopher_B._Donnan" title="Christopher B. Donnan">Christopher B. Donnan</a> in association with the <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">National Geographic Society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Skilled artisans, the Moche were a technologically advanced people who traded with faraway peoples, like the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a>. Knowledge about the Moche has been derived mostly from their ceramic pottery, which is carved with representations of their daily lives. They practiced human sacrifice, had blood-drinking rituals, and their religion incorporated non-procreative sexual practices (such as fellatio).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inca">Inca</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inca">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Holding their capital at the great puma-shaped city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuzco" title="Cuzco">Cuzco</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inca_civilization" title="Inca civilization">Inca civilization</a> dominated the Andes region from 1438 to 1533. Known as <i>Tawantin suyu</i>, or "the land of the four regions," in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" title="Quechua languages">Quechua</a>, the Inca civilization was highly distinct and developed. Inca rule extended to nearly a hundred linguistic or ethnic communities, some 9 to 14 million people connected by a 25,000-kilometre <a href="/wiki/Inca_road_system" title="Inca road system">road system</a>. Cities were built with precise, unmatched stonework, constructed over many levels of mountain terrain. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terrace_farming" title="Terrace farming">Terrace farming</a> was a useful form of agriculture. There is evidence of excellent metalwork and successful <a href="/wiki/Trepanation_in_Mesoamerica" title="Trepanation in Mesoamerica">skull surgery</a> in Inca civilization. The Inca had no written language, but used <a href="/wiki/Quipu" title="Quipu">quipu</a>, a system of knotted strings, to record information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arawak_and_Carib_civilizations">Arawak and Carib civilizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Arawak and Carib civilizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arawaks" title="Arawaks">Arawak</a> lived along the eastern coast of South America, from present-day <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guayana</a> to as far south as what is now Brazil. Explorer <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> described them at first encounter as a peaceful people, although the Arawak had already dominated other local groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Ciboney" title="Ciboney">Ciboney</a>. The Arawak had, however, come under increasing military pressure from the <a href="/wiki/Kalina_people" title="Kalina people">Carib</a>, who are believed to have left the <a href="/wiki/Orinoco" title="Orinoco">Orinoco</a> river area to settle on islands and the coast of the Caribbean Sea. Over the century leading up to Columbus' arrival in the Caribbean archipelago in 1492, the Carib are believed to have displaced many of the Arawak who previously settled the island chains. The Carib also encroached on Arawak territory in what is modern Guyana.
</p><p>The Carib were skilled boatbuilders and sailors who owed their dominance in the Caribbean basin to their military skills. The Carib war rituals included <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">cannibalism</a>; they had a practice of taking home the limbs of victims as trophies.
</p><p>It is not known how many <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_Venezuela" title="Indigenous peoples in Venezuela">indigenous peoples</a> lived in Venezuela and Colombia before the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Spanish colonization of the Americas">Spanish Conquest</a>; it may have been approximately one million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunder_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wunder-45">[45]</a></sup> including groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Auak%C3%A9_people" title="Auaké people">Auaké</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caquetio" title="Caquetio">Caquetio</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mariche" title="Mariche">Mariche</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timoto-cuicas" title="Timoto-cuicas">Timoto-cuicas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The number of people fell dramatically after the Conquest, mainly due to high mortality rates in epidemics of infectious Eurasian diseases introduced by the explorers, who carried them as an endemic disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunder_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wunder-45">[45]</a></sup> There were two main north–south axes of pre-Columbian population; producing <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> in the west and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">manioc</a> in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunder_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wunder-45">[45]</a></sup> Large parts of the <a href="/wiki/Llanos" title="Llanos">llanos</a> plains were cultivated through a combination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a> and permanent settled agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wunder_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wunder-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="European_colonization">European colonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: European colonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonial_Spanish_America" title="Colonial Spanish America">Colonial Spanish America</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Brazil" title="Colonial Brazil">Colonial Brazil</a></div>
<p>Before the arrival of Europeans 20–30 million people lived in South America.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Between 1452 and 1493, a series of papal bulls (<a href="/wiki/Dum_Diversas" title="Dum Diversas">Dum Diversas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romanus_Pontifex" title="Romanus Pontifex">Romanus Pontifex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inter_caetera" title="Inter caetera">Inter caetera</a>) paved the way for the <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">European colonization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Missionary#Catholic_missions" title="Missionary">Catholic missions</a> in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>. These authorized the European Christian nations to "take possession" of non-Christian lands and encouraged subduing and converting the non-Christian people of Africa and the Americas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DLove062007_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-DLove062007-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1494, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, the two great maritime powers of that time, signed the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a> in the expectation of new lands being discovered in the west. Through the treaty, they agreed that all the land outside Europe should be an exclusive <a href="/wiki/Duopoly" title="Duopoly">duopoly</a> between the two countries. The treaty established an imaginary line along a north–south <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(geography)" title="Meridian (geography)">meridian</a> 370 <a href="/wiki/League_(unit)" title="League (unit)">leagues</a> west of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Verde_Islands" title="Cape Verde Islands">Cape Verde Islands</a>, roughly 46° 37' W. In terms of the treaty, all land to the west of the line (which is now known to include most of the South American soil), would belong to Spain, and all land to the east, to Portugal. Because accurate measurements of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> were not possible at that time, the line was not strictly enforced, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Portuguese colonization of the Americas">Portuguese expansion of Brazil</a> across the meridian.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>
In 1498, during his third voyage to the Americas, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> sailed near the <a href="/wiki/Orinoco_Delta" title="Orinoco Delta">Orinoco Delta</a> and then landed in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Paria" title="Gulf of Paria">Gulf of Paria</a> (Actual <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>). Amazed by the great offshore current of freshwater which deflected his course eastward, Columbus expressed in his moving letter to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_Kings" title="Catholic Kings">Isabella I and Ferdinand II</a> that he must have reached heaven on Earth (terrestrial paradise): <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Great signs are these of the Terrestrial Paradise, for the site conforms to the opinion of the holy and wise theologians whom I have mentioned. And likewise, the [other] signs conform very well, for I have never read or heard of such a large quantity of fresh water being inside and in such close proximity to salt water; the very mild temperateness also corroborates this; and if the water of which I speak does not proceed from Paradise then it is an even greater marvel, because I do not believe such a large and deep river has ever been known to exist in this world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1303" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png/300px-Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png/450px-Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png/600px-Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philip_II%27s_realms_in_1598.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Union" title="Iberian Union">Iberian Union</a> in 1598, under <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a>, King of Spain and Portugal</div></div></div>
<p>Beginning in 1499, the people and natural resources of South America were repeatedly exploited by foreign <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conquistadors" title="Conquistadors">conquistadors</a>, first from Spain and later from Portugal. These competing colonial nations claimed the land and resources as their own and divided it into colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>European diseases (<a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a>) to which the native populations had no resistance were the overwhelming cause of the depopulation of the Native American population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Cruel systems of forced labor (such as <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">encomiendas</a> and mining industry's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mita_(Inca)" title="Mita (Inca)">mita</a>) under Spanish control also contributed to depopulation. Lower bound estimates speak of a decline in the population of around 20–50 percent, whereas high estimates arrive at 90 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Following this, <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">enslaved</a> Africans, who had developed immunity to these diseases, were quickly brought in to replace them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Spaniards were committed to converting their American subjects to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and were quick to purge any native cultural practices that hindered this end. However, most initial attempts at this were only partially successful; American groups simply blended <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> with their traditional beliefs. The Spaniards did not impose their language to the degree they did their religion. In fact, the missionary work of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quechua_languages" title="Quechua languages">Quechua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Guarani_language" title="Guarani language">Guarani</a> actually contributed to the expansion of these American languages, equipping them with writing systems.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Eventually, the natives and the Spaniards interbred, forming a <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">Mestizo</a> class. Mestizos and the Native Americans were often forced to pay unfair taxes to the Spanish government (although all subjects paid taxes) and were punished harshly for disobeying their laws. Many native artworks were considered pagan idols and destroyed by Spanish explorers. This included a great number of gold and silver sculptures, which were melted down before transport to Europe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="17th_and_18th_centuries">17th and 18th centuries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: 17th and 18th centuries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2015</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In 1616, the Dutch, attracted by the legend of <a href="/wiki/El_Dorado" title="El Dorado">El Dorado</a>, founded a fort in <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guayana</a> and established three colonies: <a href="/wiki/Demerara" title="Demerara">Demerara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berbice" title="Berbice">Berbice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Essequibo_(colony)" title="Essequibo (colony)">Essequibo</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1624 France attempted to settle in the area of modern-day <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a>, but was forced to abandon it in the face of hostility from the <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portuguese</a>, who viewed it as a violation of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">Treaty of Tordesillas</a>. However French settlers returned in 1630 and in 1643 managed to establish a settlement at <a href="/wiki/Cayenne" title="Cayenne">Cayenne</a> along with some small-scale plantations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Since the sixteenth century, there were some movements of discontent to the Spanish and Portuguese colonial system. Among these movements, the most famous being that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maroon_(people)" title="Maroon (people)">Maroons</a>, slaves who escaped their masters and in the shelter of the forest communities organized free communities. Attempts to subject them by the royal army were unsuccessful because the Maroons had learned to master the South American jungles. In a royal decree of 1713, the king gave legality to the first free population of the continent: Palenque de San Basilio in Colombia today, led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benkos_Bioho" title="Benkos Bioho">Benkos Bioho</a>. Brazil saw the formation of a genuine African kingdom on their soil, with the <a href="/wiki/Palmares_(quilombo)" title="Palmares (quilombo)">Quilombo of Palmares</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Between 1721 and 1735, the <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Comuneros_(Paraguay)" title="Revolt of the Comuneros (Paraguay)">Revolt of the Comuneros of Paraguay</a> arose, because of clashes between the Paraguayan settlers and the Jesuits, who ran the large and prosperous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit_Reductions" title="Jesuit Reductions">Jesuit Reductions</a> and controlled a large number of Christianized Natives.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Between 1742 and 1756, was the insurrection of <a href="/wiki/Juan_Santos_Atahualpa" title="Juan Santos Atahualpa">Juan Santos Atahualpa</a> in the central jungle of <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>. In 1780, the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_Peru" title="Viceroyalty of Peru">Viceroyalty of Peru</a> was met with the insurrection of curaca <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BApac_Amaru_II" title="Túpac Amaru II">Joseph Gabriel Condorcanqui</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tupac_Amaru_II" title="Tupac Amaru II">Tupac Amaru II</a>, which would be continued by Tupac Katari in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peru" title="Upper Peru">Upper Peru</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 1763, the African <a href="/wiki/Coffy_(person)" title="Coffy (person)">Coffy</a> led a revolt in Guyana which was bloodily suppressed by the Dutch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
In 1781, the <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Comuneros_(New_Granada)" title="Revolt of the Comuneros (New Granada)">Revolt of the Comuneros (New Granada)</a>,  an insurrection of the villagers in the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">Viceroyalty of New Granada</a>, was a popular revolution that united indigenous people and mestizos. The villagers tried to be the colonial power and despite the capitulation were signed, the Viceroy <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Antonio_Fl%C3%B3rez" title="Manuel Antonio Flórez">Manuel Antonio Flórez</a> did not comply, and instead ran to the main leaders <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Antonio_Gal%C3%A1n" title="José Antonio Galán">José Antonio Galán</a>.
</p><p>In 1796, the Dutch colony of <a href="/wiki/Essequibo_(colony)" title="Essequibo (colony)">Essequibo</a> was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">British</a> during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">French Revolutionary Wars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the eighteenth century, the figure of the priest, mathematician and botanist <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Celestino_Mutis" title="José Celestino Mutis">José Celestino Mutis</a> (1732–1808), was delegated by the Viceroy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonio_Caballero_y_Gongora" title="Antonio Caballero y Gongora">Antonio Caballero y Gongora</a> to conduct an inventory of the nature of the <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">Nueva Granada</a>, which became known as the Botanical Expedition, which classified plants, wildlife and founded the first astronomical observatory in the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santa_F%C3%A9_de_Bogot%C3%A1" title="Santa Fé de Bogotá">Santa Fé de Bogotá</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>On August 15, 1801, the Prussian scientist <a href="/wiki/Alexander_von_Humboldt" title="Alexander von Humboldt">Alexander von Humboldt</a> reached Fontibón where Mutis had begun his expedition to <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Granada" title="Viceroyalty of New Granada">New Granada</a>, Quito. The meeting between the two scholars is considered the brightest spot of the botanical expedition.
Humboldt also visited Venezuela, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, Chile, and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>.
Through his observations of temperature differences between the Pacific Ocean between Chile and Peru in different periods of the year, he discovered cold currents moving from south to north up the coast of Peru, which was named the Humboldt Current in his honor.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Between 1806 and 1807, British military forces <a href="/wiki/British_invasions_of_the_River_Plate" title="British invasions of the River Plate">tried to invade the area</a> of the Rio de la Plata, at the command of <a href="/wiki/Home_Riggs_Popham" title="Home Riggs Popham">Home Riggs Popham</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Carr_Beresford" title="William Carr Beresford">William Carr Beresford</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_Whitelocke" title="John Whitelocke">John Whitelocke</a>. The invasions were repelled, but powerfully affected the Spanish authority.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_and_19th_century">Independence and 19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_South_America&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Independence and 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1250" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG/220px-Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG/330px-Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG/440px-Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latin_American_independence_countries.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Countries in the Americas by date of independence</div></div></div>
<p>The Spanish colonies won their independence in the first quarter of the 19th century, in the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_American_wars_of_independence" title="Spanish American wars of independence">Spanish American wars of independence</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sim%C3%B3n_Bol%C3%ADvar" title="Simón Bolívar">Simón Bolívar</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Colombia" title="Greater Colombia">Greater Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>), <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_San_Mart%C3%ADn" title="José de San Martín">José de San Martín</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_the_River_Plate" title="United Provinces of the River Plate">United Provinces of the River Plate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Bernardo_O%27Higgins" title="Bernardo O'Higgins">Bernardo O'Higgins</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>) led their independence struggle. Although Bolivar attempted to keep the Spanish-speaking parts of the continent politically unified, they rapidly became independent of one another.
</p><p>Unlike the Spanish colonies, the Brazilian independence came as an indirect consequence of the Napoleonic Invasions to Portugal – French invasion under General Junot led to the capture of <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> on 8 December 1807. In order not to lose its sovereignty, the Portuguese Court moved the capital from <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">Lisbon</a> to <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, which was the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>'s capital between 1808 and 1821 and rose the relevance of <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese Empire</a>'s framework. Following the Portuguese <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Revolution_of_1820" title="Liberal Revolution of 1820">Liberal Revolution of 1820</a>, and after several battles and skirmishes were fought in Pará and in Bahia, the <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> <a href="/wiki/Pedro_I_of_Brazil" title="Pedro I of Brazil">Pedro</a>, son of King <a href="/wiki/John_VI_of_Portugal" title="John VI of Portugal">John VI of Portugal</a>, proclaimed the country's independence in 1822 and became Brazil's first <a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">emperor</a> (He later also reigned as Pedro IV of Portugal). This was one of the most peaceful colonial independences ever seen in human history.
</p>
